    Section I: Group Theory

    Problem F.166. Problem 1.

    Let \(G\) be a group (not necessarily finite) and \(H\) a nonempty subset of \(G\) that is [[Mathematics/Definitions/Multiplicatively Closed Set|closed under multiplication]]. Suppose that for all \(g\in G\) we have \(g^2\in H\text{.}\) Prove the following: (a) \(H\) is a subgroup of \(G\) (b) \(H\) is normal (c) \(G/H\) (See: [[Mathematics/Definitions/Coset]]) is abelian.
    Solution.
    Proof. Let \(G\) be a group, \(H\) a multiplicatively closed subset of \(G\text{,}\) and suppose that \(g^2\in H\) for all \(g\in G\text{.}\)
    Part (a) First, notice that \(e^2=e\in H\text{.}\) Let \(x\in H\text{,}\) and consider \(x\inv\text{.}\) Notice that \((x{\inv})^2=x^{-2}\in H\text{.}\) As \(H\) is multiplicatively closed, we see that \(xx^{-2}=x\inv\in H\text{.}\) Thus \(H\) is a subgroup of \(G\) by the [[Lemma ‚Äì Subgroup Tests|subgroup test]].
    Part (b) Let \(g\in G\text{,}\) \(h\in H\text{,}\) and consider \(gh\text{.}\) Notice that \((gh)^2=ghgh\in H\text{.}\) Multiplying by \(h\inv\) on the right we see \(ghg\in H\text{,}\) as it is multiplicatively closed and \(h\inv\in H\text{.}\) We rewrite \(ghg=ghggg\inv=ghg\inv g^2\text{,}\) given that elements always commute with their inverses. As \(g^2\in H\text{,}\) we see that \(ghg\inv\in H\) as well. Thus \(H\) is normal in \(G\text{.}\)
    Part (c) Let \(x,y\in G/H\text{.}\) As \(g^2\in H\) for every \(g\in G\text{,}\) every element \(G/H\) has order \(2\text{.}\) Thus \(xyxy=e\) and so \(xy=(xy)\inv=yx\text{,}\) making the group abelian.

    Problem F.167. Problem 2.

    Construct a nonabelian group of order \(21\) and give a [[Mathematics/Definitions/Presentation|presentation]] for your group (with justification).
    Solution.
    First, we know that \(G=C_{21}\) is a group of order \(21\text{.}\) By [[Theorem ‚Äì Cauchy's Theorem|Cauchy's Theorem]], as \(3\) and \(7\) are primes dividing the order of \(G\) there exist elements \(k,h\) of orders \(3\text{,}\) \(7\text{,}\) respectively. Consider \(K=\langle k\rangle\) and \(H=\langle h\rangle\text{,}\) both of which are subgroups of \(C_{21}\text{.}\) By [[Theorem ‚Äì Lagrange's Theorem|Lagrange's Theorem]] we see \([C_{21}:H]=3\) (See: [[Mathematics/Definitions/Index]]), the smallest prime dividing \(21\text{,}\) making \(H\) normal in \(G.\) Thus \(HK\leq G\text{,}\) but as \(K\cap H=\{e\}\) we have \(|HK|=21=|G|\) and thus that \(HK=G\text{.}\) By the [[Proposition ‚Äì Recognition Theorem Internal Semidirect Products|Recognition Theorem]] we have \(H\sdp_\rho K\cong G\text{,}\) where \(\rho:K\to\Aut(H)\) is the [[Proposition ‚Äì Permutation Representation|permutation representation]] of the action of \(K\) on \(H\) via automorphisms given by conjugation in \(G\text{.}\)

    Problem F.168. Problem 3.

    Let \(G\) be a group and let \(n_p\) be the number of Sylow \(p\)-subgroups of \(G\text{,}\) where \(p\) is a prime dividing the order of \(G\text{.}\) (a) Prove that if \(G\) is simple then \(|G|\big|n_p!\) (b) Deduce that there is no simple group of order \(1,000,000\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    Proof. Let \(G\) be a group, \(p\) a prime dividing the order of \(G\text{,}\) and \(n_p\) the number of Sylow \(p\)-subgroups of \(G\text{.}\)
    Part (a) Let \(G\) act on \(\Syl_p(G)\) by conjugation, inducing the homomorphism \(\rho: G\to S_{n_p}\) via the [[Proposition ‚Äì Permutation Representation|permutation representation]]. Notice that the order of \(S_{n_p}\) is conspicuously \(n_p!\text{.}\) The kernel of this map is a normal subgroup of \(G\) (See: [[Corollary ‚Äì Normal iff Kernel of Homomorphism|Corollary]]). Note that since \(G\) is simple the only normal subgroups of \(G\) are the trivial subgroup and \(G\) itself. However, the kernel cannot be all of \(G\) as this would make \(\rho\) trivial, which cannot be the case given that our action is transitive by Part (2) of Sylow's Theorem. Thus \(\ker(\rho)=\{e\}\text{,}\) making \(\rho\) injective. Thus \(|\im(\rho)|=|G|\text{.}\) As the [[Mathematics/Definitions/Image|image]] is a subgroup of \(S_{n_p}\text{,}\) the result follows from [[Theorem ‚Äì Lagrange's Theorem|Lagrange's Theorem]].
    Part (b) Let \(G\) be a group of order \(1,000,000\text{.}\) Suppose by way of contradiction that \(G\) is simple. \(1,000,000=10^6=2^6\cdot 5^6\text{.}\) Thus the number of Sylow-\(5\) subgroups is congruent to \(1\mod 5\) and divides \(2^6=64\text{,}\) the options of which are 1 and 16 (See: Sylow's Theorem). As \(|G|\) does not divide \(16!,\) this contradicts part (a). Thus there are no simple groups of order \(1,000,000\text{.}\)
    Section II:

    Problem F.169. Problem 4.

    1. Suppose that \(A\) is an integral domain that contains a field \(F\) as a [[Mathematics/Definitions/Subring|subring]], and that moreover \(A\) is finite dimensional as an \(F\)-vector space. Prove that \(A\) is a field.
    2. Give an example to show that the finite dimension condition is necessary.
    Solution.
    Proof. Part (a) Let \(A\) be an integral domain that contains a field \(F\) as a subring, and that moreover \(A\) is finite dimensional as an \(F\)-vector space.
    Say ùê¥ has dimension \(n\) over \(K\text{.}\) Let \(x\in A,x\neq0\text{.}\) Consider elements \(1,x,x^2,x^{3,\dots,}\) which cannot be independent because of the finite dimension. So we may choose \(m\) so that \(1,x,\dots,x^m\) is linearly dependent over \(K\) and is as small as possible. This means that we may find \(c_0,c_1,\dots,c^{m}\in K\text{,}\) not all equal to \(0\text{,}\) such that \(c_0+c_1x+\dots+c_mx^m=0\text{.}\) Note that \(c_0\neq0\text{,}\) as this would contradict the minimality of \(m\text{.}\) Then \(x(c_1+\dots+c)_mx^{m-1})\) is invertible, so ùë• is invertible as well, making \(A\) a field.
    Part (b) Consider \(\Q[x]\text{,}\) which contains the subring \(\Q\text{.}\) However, this is not a finite dimensional vector space over \(F\text{,}\) and \(\Q[x]\) is not a field, as \(x\) has no inverse.

    Problem F.170. Problem 5.

    Suppose that \(T : V\to V\) is a [[Mathematics/Definitions/Linear Transformation|linear map]], where \(V\) is a finite dimensional \(\C\)-vector space. Fix a polynomial \(p\in \C[x]\text{.}\)
    1. Prove that if \(\lambda\) is an [[Mathematics/Definitions/Eigenvalue|eigenvalue]] of \(T\) then \(p(\lambda)\) is an eigenvalue of \(p(T)\text{.}\)
    2. Prove conversely that if \(\mu\) is an eigenvalue of \(p(T)\) then there exists an eigenvalue \(\l\) of \(T\) such that \(\mu = p(\l)\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    Part (a) Let \(\l\) be an eigenvalue of \(T\text{.}\) Thus there exists some \(v\) such that \(Tv=\l v\text{.}\) Notice that \(p(T)v=a_nT^nv+\dots+a_0Iv=0\) and \(p(\l)=a_n\l^n+\dots+a_0\text{.}\) Then
    \begin{equation*} \begin{align}(p(T)-p(\l I))v&=p(T)v-p(\l I)v\\&=(a_nT^nv+\dots+a_0Iv)-(a_n\l^nv+\dots+a_0v)\\&=a_n(T^n-\l^n)v+\dots a_0(I-I)v\\&=0,\end{align} \end{equation*}
    making \(p(\l)\) an eigenvalue of \(p(T)\text{.}\)
    Part (b) First note that if \(T\) is a scalar of the identity matrix then its only eigenvalue is \(0\text{.}\) Let \(\mu\) be an eigenvalue of \(p(T)\text{.}\) Thus \((p(T)-\mu I)v=0\) for some \(v\text{.}\) As \(\C\) is algebraically closed we can factor the polynomial \(p(x)-\mu\) into linear terms:
    \begin{equation*} p(x)-\mu=c(x-r_1)\dots(x-r_n) \end{equation*}
    for roots \(r_i\text{.}\) Note that \(p(r_i)-\mu=0\) for all \(r_i\text{.}\) Observe
    \begin{equation*} \begin{align}(p(T)-\mu I)v&=p(T)v-(\mu I)v\\&=c(T-r_nI)\dots(T-r_1I)v\\&=0.\end{align} \end{equation*}
    Thus one of these terms must be sent to zero. Note that if \(T-r_iI=0\) for any \(I\) this would make \(T\) a scaler of the identity matrix. Thus there exists some \(i\) such that \((T-r_iI)v=0\text{,}\) making \(r_i\) an eigenvalue of \(T\text{.}\) Notice that as \(p(r_i)-\mu=0\) we have \(p(r_i)=\mu\text{,}\) completing the proof.

    Problem F.171. Problem 6.

    Consider the \(\C\)-vector space \(V = {p \in \C[x] : \deg(p) \leq n - 1}\text{.}\) (You may assume without proof that \(V\) is n-[[Mathematics/Definitions/Dimension (Vector Space)|dimensional]].) Consider the following [[Mathematics/Definitions/Linear Operator|linear maps]] \(V \to V\text{:}\)
    \begin{equation*} D : V \to V \hspace{4cm} T : V \to V \end{equation*}
    \begin{equation*} p\mapsto p'\hspace{4cm} p\mapsto xp' \end{equation*}
    (where p' denotes the [[Mathematics/Definitions/Derivative|derivative]] of p). Determine the [[Theorem ‚Äì Jordan Canonical Form|Jordan normal form]] of (a) \(D\) (b) \(B\)
    Solution.
    Part (a) For any integer \(n \geq 1\text{,}\) consider the \(\C\)-vector space \(V = \{p \in \C[x] : \deg(p) \leq n - 1\}\text{.}\)
    Let \(T:V \to V\) be the [[Mathematics/Definitions/Linear Operator|linear operator]] given by \(T(p) = xp'\) (where \(p'\) denotes the derivative of \(p\)). Note that the [[Mathematics/Definitions/Change of Basis Matrix|change of basis]] matrix for this operator is given by
    \begin{equation*} \begin{bmatrix} 0 & 0 & \cdots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 & \cdots & 0 & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots \\ 0 & 0 & \cdots & 0 & n-1 \\ \end{bmatrix}, \end{equation*}
    with the basis \(\{1,x,\dots,x^{n-1}\}\text{.}\) Thus \(\cp_A(x)\) will be given by the [[Determinant|determinant]] of the matrix
    \begin{equation*} \begin{bmatrix} x & 0 & \cdots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & x-1 & \cdots & 0 & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots \\ 0 & 0 & \cdots & 0 & x-(n-1) \\ \end{bmatrix}, \end{equation*}
    which is [[Mathematics/Definitions/Diagonal Matrix|diagonal]]. Hence
    \begin{equation*} \cp_AT(x)=\displaystyle\prod_{i=0}^{n-1}(x-i). \end{equation*}
    Thus \(\cp_AT(x)\) factors into distinct linear polynomials, each of which is in the form \((x-i)\) for \(0\leq i\leq n-1\text{.}\) Thus each linear term is an elementary divisor, making each [[Mathematics/Definitions/Jordan Block|Jordan block]] a \(1\times1\) matrix with \(i\) as the only entry. Thus the Jordan Canonical form is
    \begin{equation*} \begin{bmatrix} 0 & 0 & \cdots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 & \cdots & 0 & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots \\ 0 & 0 & \cdots & 0 & n-1 \\ \end{bmatrix}. \end{equation*}
    Part (b) This time around the change of basis matrix (denoted \(B\) and using the same basis as above) for this matrix has 0s along the diagonal, and increasing natural numbers (starting at 0, sorry) along the upper diagonal. Thus \(\cp_B(x)=x^n\text{.}\)
    Recall that the minimal polynomial corresponding to \(B\) will be the the smallest monic polynomial such that \(B\) is sent to 0. Note that as \(D\) is the operator sending \(p\) to its derivative, and that \(D^{(2)}=D(D(p))=p''=p^{(2)}\text{.}\) Thus \(B^2\) can be viewed as a change of basis matrix for taking the second derivative of the basis, and so on.
    As the basis extends to \(x^{n-1}\text{,}\) it requires \(n\) derivatives to make this polynomial become 0. Thus the minimal polynomial of \(B\) must be \(x^n\text{,}\) as it it monic and \(B^n=0\text{.}\) As the degree of the invariant factors must sum to \(n\) and \(\mp_B(x)=x^n\text{,}\) which is itself an invariant factor, we see that it must in fact be the only one.
    As \(x^n\) is already a power of a prime, it is the only elementary divisor as well. Thus the Jordan Canonical Form for \(B\) is an \(n\times n\) Jordan Block with 0s along the diagonal and 1s along the sub-diagonal.
    Section III:

    Problem F.172. Problem 7.

    Assume that \(F \sse K\) is a finite extension of fields of degree \(n = [K : F ]\text{.}\)
    1. Prove that if \(f \in F [x]\) is irreducible of degree \(d\) and \(\gcd(d, n) = 1\) then \(f\) remains irreducible when regarded as an element of the ring \(K[x]\text{.}\)
    2. Show, by means of an explicit example with justification, that the statement in part (a) would become false if the assumption that \(\gcd(n, d) = 1\) were omitted.
    Solution.
    Proof. Let \(F \subseteq K\) be a finite extension of fields of degree \(n = [K : F ]\text{.}\)
    Part (a) Suppose that \(f \in F[x]\) is irreducible of degree \(d\) and \(\gcd(d, n) = 1\text{.}\)
    First, note that if \(d=1\) then \(f(x)\) will remain irreducible in \(K[x]\) (See: [[Theorem ‚Äì Degree and Irreducibility|Theorem]]). Suppose then that \(d>1\text{.}\) There exists an algebraically closed extension \(\widetilde F\) such that \(f\) has a root \(r\) (See: [[Theorem ‚Äì Existence and Uniqueness of Algebraic Closures|Theorem]]). Consider \(K(r)\text{.}\) As \(r\) is algebraic in \(K(r)\) we know there exists some unique irreducible minimal polynomial \(g\) of degree \(q\text{,}\) and thus that \([K(r):K]=q\) (See: [[Theorem ‚Äì Properties of Algebraic Elements|Theorem]]). Using the [[Proposition ‚Äì The Degree Formula|Degree Formula]] we see that
    \begin{equation*} [K(r):F]=[K(r):K][K:F]=qn. \end{equation*}
    However, \([K(r):F]=[K(r):F(r)][F(r):F]\) and so \(qn=md\) for some \(m\in\Z\text{,}\) so \(d|qn\text{.}\) As \(\gcd(n,d)=1\) we must have \(d|q\text{.}\) But \(q\) was defined to be the degree of \(g\text{,}\) which divides \(f\text{.}\) As \(d|q\) and \(q\leq d\text{,}\) we see that \(d=q\text{,}\) so so \(f=kg\) for some \(k\in F\text{.}\) As irreducible polynomials multiplied by a constant are still irreducible, we see that \(f\) is indeed irreducible in \(K[x]\text{.}\)
    Part (b) Let \(p(x)\) be a non-constant irreducible polynomial of degree \(d\) in \(F[x]\text{.}\) Let \(K=F[x]/p(x)\text{.}\) Because \(p(x)\) is irreducible and \(F[x]\) is a PID, \((p(x))\) is a maximal ideal. Thus \(K = F[x]/(p(x))\) is a field (See: [[Theorem ‚Äì Prime, Maximal, Domain, Field|Theorem]]), \([K:F]=d\text{,}\) and \(x+p(x)\) is a root of \(p(x)\) in \(K\) (See: [[Proposition ‚Äì Properties of Extension Degrees|Proposition]]). Hence \(p(x)\) is no longer irreducible by Theorem 2.2.

    Problem F.173. Problem 8.

    Suppose that \(F \sse L\) is a finite Galois extension with Galois group \(G\text{,}\) and that \(\a \in L\text{.}\) Prove that \(L = F (\a)\) if and only if the images of \(\a\) under elements of \(G\) are distinct.
    Solution.
    First, suppose that \(L=F(\a)\text{.}\) As \(L\) is a Galois extension the minimum polynomial of \(\a\) in \(F\) splits completely into linear factors (See: [[Theorem ‚Äì Galois Extension Equivalencies|Theorem]]). Thus \(G\) acts faithfully on the roots of \(\mp_\a(x)\text{,}\) which includes \(\a\) (See: [[Corollary ‚Äì Automorphisms and Group Actions (Fields)|Corollary]]). Thus the images of \(\a\) under elements of \(G\) are distinct.
    Now suppose that the images of \(\a\) under elements of \(G\) are distinct, and suppose by way of contradiction that there exists some \(\b\in L\) that is not in \(F(\a)\text{.}\) Consider the intermediate field \(F(\a,\b)\text{.}\) By the [[Mathematics/Modern Algebra/Results/Theorem ‚Äì Fundamental Theorem of Galois Theory|FTGT]] there exists a nontrivial subgroup of \(G\) whose elements fix elements of \(F(\a,\b)\text{,}\) including \(\a\text{,}\) a contradiction.

    Problem F.174. Problem 9.

    Let \(p = x^6 + 3 \in \Q[x]\text{,}\) and let \(L\) be its splitting field over \(\Q\text{.}\) Prove that if \(\a\) is a root of \(p\) then \(L = \Q(\a)\text{.}\) Hence (or otherwise) determine \([L : Q]\text{.}\) (Hint: Consider the value of \(\a_3\text{.}\))
    Solution.
    Let \(p = x^6 + 3 \in \Q[x]\text{,}\) and let \(L\) be its splitting field over \(\Q\) and suppose \(\a\) is a root of \(p\text{.}\) Note that \((\frac{\a^3+1}{2})^2=\frac{\a^3-1}{2}\text{,}\) and so
    \begin{equation*} \left(\frac{\a^3+1}{2}\right)^6=\left(\left(\frac{\a^3+1}{2}\right)^2\right)^3=\left(\frac{\a^3-1}{2}\right)^3=1, \end{equation*}
    making \(\a\) a primitive sixth root of unity. By squaring primitive sixth roots of unity over and over we get all of the other sixth roots of unity as well, and so \(e^{2\pi i/6}\in L\text{.}\) Thus we have all the roots of \(p\text{,}\) and so \(L=\Q(a)\) and \([L:\Q]=6\text{.}\)

    May 2022.

    Section I: Group Theory

    Problem F.175. Problem 1.

    Let \(G\) be a finite \(p\)-group for some prime \(p\) and \(N\neq {1}\) a normal subgroup of \(G\text{.}\) Prove that \(N\cap Z(G)\neq{1}\text{,}\) where \(Z(G)\) is the center of \(G\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    Proof. Let \(G\) be a finite \(p\)-group for some prime \(p\) and \(N\neq {1}\) a normal subgroup of \(G\text{.}\)
    First, we show that the center of \(G\) is nontrivial. (See: [[Corollary ‚Äì Center of \(p\)-group is Nontrivial|Corollary]]) Suppose by way of contradiction that \(Z(G)=\{e\}\text{.}\) We examine the conjugacy classes of \(G\text{.}\) From the [[Theorem ‚Äì The Class Equation|Class Equation]], we know
    \begin{equation*} |G| = |Z(G)| + \sum_i^r |G : C_G(g_i)|. \end{equation*}
    Note that \(|G|=p^n\) for some \(n\in\N\text{,}\) meaning that the only divisors of \(|G|\) are powers of \(p\text{.}\) In finite groups, each conjugacy class must [[Corollary ‚Äì \(C_G(g_i) big G\) for Finite Groups|divide]] the order of the group. By [[Theorem ‚Äì Lagrange's Theorem|Lagrange's Theorem]], \(|G : C_G(g_i)|=\frac{|G|}{|C_G(g_i)|}=\frac{p^n}{p^m}=p^k\text{,}\) where \(m,k\in\N\text{.}\) Since |Z(G)|=1, we see that \(\sum_i^r |G : C_G(g_i)|\bigg|p^n-1\text{,}\) which is impossible given that \(p^{n-1} \not\mid p^n\text{.}\) Thus \(Z(G)\neq\{e\}\text{.}\)
    As \(N\nsg G\) it is a [[Mathematics/Modern Algebra/Results/Corollary ‚Äì Disjoint Union of Conjugacy Classes|union]] of conjugacy classes of the elements it contains, one of which is \(e\text{.}\) Assume by way of contradiction that \(N\cap Z(G)={e}\text{,}\) meaning that \(e\) is the only element in \(N\) whose conjugacy class is a singleton. This yields
    \begin{equation*} |N| = 1+ \sum_i^r |G : C_G(g_i)|. \end{equation*}
    However, by Lagrange's Theorem \(N\) must also be a \(p-\)group, and thus by an analogous element counting argument as above we see that there exists some \(x\in N\) such that \(C_G(x)=\{x\}\text{,}\) or that \(gxg\inv=x\) for all \(g\in G\text{.}\) Thankfully, this means that \(x\in Z(G)\text{,}\) and thus we have \(Z(G)\cap N\neq 1\text{.}\)

    Problem F.176. Problem 2.

    A non-abelian group of order \(27\text{.}\) (a) Prove there exists a non-abelian group of \(27\text{.}\) (Hint: Use a semi-direct product.) (b) Find, with justification, a presentation of the group you found in part (a).
    Solution.
    1. Let \(C_3 = \langle a \rangle\) be the cyclic group of order \(3\text{,}\) and let \(C_9 = \langle b \rangle\) be the cyclic group of order \(9\text{.}\) Let \(\phi \colon C_9 \to \operatorname{Aut}(C_3)\) be the homomorphism defined by \(\phi(b^i)(a) = a^i\) for \(0 \le i \le 8\text{,}\) where we identify \(\operatorname{Aut}(C_3)\) with \(C_2\) via the isomorphism \(\operatorname{Aut}(C_3) \cong C_2\) given by sending the non-identity automorphism to the generator of \(C_2\text{.}\)
    Then the semi-direct product \(G = C_3 \rtimes_{\phi} C_9\) has order \(27\) and is non-abelian. To see that \(G\) is non-abelian, note that \(bab^{-1} \neq a\) for any \(b \in C_9 \setminus {1}\text{,}\) since \(a\) has no non-trivial automorphisms.
    1. To find a presentation of \(G\text{,}\) we need to identify the generators and relations. Since \(C_3\) and \(C_9\) are both cyclic, we can take the generators to be \(a\) and \(b\) respectively. Moreover, since \(C_3\) is normal in \(G\text{,}\) we have \(bab^{-1} = a^i\) for some \(i \in {1, 2}\text{.}\)
    To determine the value of \(i\text{,}\) note that \(C_9\) acts on \(C_3\) by automorphisms, and the non-identity automorphism of \(C_3\) is inversion \(a \mapsto a^{-1}\text{.}\) Since \(bab^{-1} \neq a^{-1}\text{,}\) we must have \(i = 2\text{.}\)
    Therefore, a presentation of \(G\) is given by \(\langle a, b \mid a^3 = b^9 = 1, bab^{-1} = a^2\rangle\text{.}\)

    Problem F.177. Problem 3.

    Let G be a group of order \(2^5\cdot 7^3\text{.}\) Prove that \(G\) is not simple.
    Solution.
    #Proof. By Sylow's Theorem we know that \(\Syl_7(G)\equiv 1\mod{7}\) and \(\Syl_7(G)|2^5=32\text{.}\) Thus our options are \(1\) and \(8\text{.}\) Suppose that \(\Syl_7(G)=8\text{.}\)
    Let \(G\) act on \(\Syl_7(G)\) by conjugation, yielding the homomorphism
    \begin{equation*} \rho: G \to S_8 \end{equation*}
    via the [[Proposition ‚Äì Permutation Representation|permutation representation]]. This map is non-trivial from part (2) of Sylow's Theorem, but \(2^5\cdot 7^3\) does not divide \(|S_8|=8!\text{,}\) and thus \(\rho\) cannot be injective. Then the kernel of this homomorphism is non-trivial, normal subgroup of \(G\) (See: [[Corollary ‚Äì Normal iff Kernel of Homomorphism|Result]]). Thus \(G\) is not simple.

    Problem F.178. Problem 4.

    Let \(R\) be a commutative ring with identity, and assume \(1\neq0\text{.}\) Let \(I\) and \(J\) be ideals such that \(I + J = R\text{.}\)
    1. Prove \(IJ = I\cap J\text{.}\)
    2. Prove the following special case of the Sunzi's Remainder Theorem: There is an isomorphism of rings of the form \(R/(I \cap J)\cong R/I \times R/J\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    Proof. Let \(R\) be a commutative ring with identity, and assume \(1\neq0\text{.}\) Let \(I\) and \(J\) be ideals such that \(I + J = R\text{.}\)
    Part (a) First, note that both \(IJ\) and \(I\cap J\) are ideals (See: [[Proposition ‚Äì Combinations of Ideals|Result]]).
    Let \(x\in IJ\text{.}\) Thus \(x=\sum a_ib_j\text{,}\) where each \(a_i\in I\) and \(b_j\in J\text{.}\) As \(I\) and \(J\) are both ideals, each term in this sum is contained both in \(I\) and \(J\text{.}\) Thus, by absorption, \(x\in I\cap J\text{.}\) Hence \(IJ\subseteq I\cap J\text{.}\)
    Let \(x\in I\cap J\text{.}\) Thus \(x\in I\) and \(x\in J\text{.}\) Note that as \(I+J=R\text{,}\) there exists some \(a\in I\) and \(b\in J\) such that \(a+b=1\text{.}\) So \(x=x1=xa+xb\text{.}\) As \(x\in I\cap J\) we see that \(x=ax+xb\text{,}\) with \(a,x\in I\) and \(b,x\in J\text{.}\) Thus \(x\in IJ\text{,}\) yielding \(IJ=I\cap J\text{.}\)
    Part (b)
    Let \(f:R\to R/I\times R/J\) be defined by \(f(x)=(x+I,x+J)\text{.}\)
    Notice that if \(x\in I\cap J\text{,}\) we have \(f(x)=(I,J)\text{,}\) and so \(x\in\Ker(f)\text{.}\) Let \(x\in\Ker(f)\text{.}\) Thus \(x+I=I\) and \(x+J=J\text{,}\) and so \(x\in I\) and \(x\in J\text{.}\) Hence \(x\in I\cap J\text{,}\) and so \(\Ker(f)=I\cap J\text{.}\)
    Observe
    \begin{equation*} f(x+y)=((x+y)+I,(x+y)+J)=((x+I)+(y+I),(x+J)+(y+J))=((x+I),(x+J))+(y+I),(y+J))=f(x)+f(y), \end{equation*}
    making \(f\) a homomorphism of rings.
    Let \((a+I,b+J)\in R/I\times R/J\text{.}\) As \(R=I+J\text{,}\) we can write \(a\) and \(b\) as \(a_i+a_j\) and \(b_i+b_j\text{.}\) However, as \(a_i\in I\) and \(b_j\in J\text{,}\) we have \((a+I,b+J)=(a_j+I, b_i+J)\text{.}\)
    Consider the element \(x=(a_j+b_i)\in R\text{,}\) and observe
    \begin{equation*} f(x)=f(a_j+b_i)=(a_j+b_i+I, b_i+a_j+J). \end{equation*}
    However, as \(b_i\in I\) and \(a_j\in J\text{,}\) we see
    \begin{equation*} (a_j+b_i+I, b_i+a_j+J)=(a_j+I,b_i+J)=(a+I,b+J). \end{equation*}
    Thus \(f\) is a surjective homomorphism. Hence, by the First Isomorphism Theorem, we see \(R/(I \cap J)\cong R/I \times R/J\text{.}\)

    \begin{equation*} \rank(g) + \nullity(g) = \dim(V). \end{equation*}
    (Recall the rank of \(g\) is the dimension of its image and the nullity of \(g\) is the dimension of its kernel.)

    Problem F.180. Problem 6.

    Let \(F\) be a field and \(n\) a positive integer. We say an \(n\times n\) matrix \(A\) with entries in \(F\) is unipotent if \(A-In\) is [[Mathematics/Definitions/Nilpotent|nilpotent]] (i.e., \((A-In)^k = 0\) for some \(k \geq 1\)). For the field \(F = \Q\text{,}\) find (with complete justification) the number of similarity classes of \(4\times 4\) unipotent matrices and give an explicit representative for each class.
    Solution.
    Let \(F=\Q\text{,}\) \(n\) a positive integer, and \(A\) a unipotent \(4\times 4\) matrix with entries in \(F\text{.}\) Thus \(A-I\) is nilpotent. Let \(\l\) be an eigenvalue of \(A-I\text{.}\) Then \((A-I)v=\l v\text{,}\) so \(Av-Iv=\l v\) and \(Av=\l v+Iv\text{.}\) As \(Iv=v\text{,}\) we have \(Av=\l v+v\) and \(Av=(\l+1)v\text{.}\)
    Notice that as \(\l\) is an eigenvalue of \(I\text{,}\) we have \(\l +1\) as an eigenvalue of \(A\text{.}\)
    Assume inductively that \(\l^{n-1}\) is an eigenvalue of \((A-I)^{n-1}\text{.}\) Notice
    \begin{equation*} (A+I)^nv=(A+I)(A+I)^{n-1}v=(A+I)\l^{n-1}v=\l^{n-1}(A+I)v=\l^{n-1}\l=\l^n, \end{equation*}
    making \(\l\) an eigenvalue of \((A+I)^n\text{.}\) Thus if \(\l\) is an eigenvalue of \(A-I\text{,}\) it is an eigenvalue of \((A-I)^n\) as well. As \((A-I)\) is nilpotent, there exists some \(k\) such that \((A-I)^k=0\text{.}\) This means that \(\l^kv=0\text{.}\) As \(v\neq 0\) and \(\l^k\) is a scalar in a field (and hence integral domain) we have \(\l=0\text{.}\) Thus the only eigenvalue of \((A-I)\) is \(0\text{,}\) meaning that the only eigenvalue of \(A\) is \(1\text{.}\)
    Eigenvalues of \(A\) correspond to the roots of \(\cp_A(x)\) (See: [[Mathematics/Definitions/Characteristic Polynomial|CharPoly]]), which is a monic quartic polynomial, as \(A\) is a \(4\times 4\) matrix. Thus \(\cp_A(x)=(x-1)^4\text{,}\) as all roots must be \(1\text{.}\)
    Two matrices are similar if and only if they share the same [[Mathematics/Definitions/Invariant Factor|invariant factors]]. Given that invariant factors divide \(\cp_A(x)\) and each invariant factor must divide the following one, the possible sets of invariant factors for \(A\) are the following: - \(\{(x-1)^4\}\text{,}\) - \(\{(x-1),(x-1)^3\}\text{,}\) - \(\{(x-1), (x-1), (x-1)^2\}\text{,}\) - \(\{(x-1)^2, (x-1)^2\}\text{,}\) and - \(\{(x-1), (x-1), (x-1), (x-1)\}\text{.}\) We identify the companion matrices for each possible invariant factor:
    \begin{equation*} C(x-1)=\begin{bmatrix} 1 \end{bmatrix}, \end{equation*}
    \begin{equation*} C((x-1)^2)=C(x^2-2x+1)=\begin{bmatrix} 0 & -1 \\ 1 & 2\end{bmatrix}, \end{equation*}
    \begin{equation*} C((x-1)^3)=C(x^3+3x^2+3x-1)=\begin{bmatrix} 0 & 0 & 1 \\ 1 & 0 & -3 \\ 0 & 1 & -3\end{bmatrix}, \end{equation*}
    and
    \begin{equation*} C((x-1)^4)=C(x^4-4x^3+6x^2-4x+1)=\begin{bmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 & -1 \\ 1 & 0 & 0 & 4 \\ 0 & 1 & 0 & -6 \\ 0 & 0 & 1 & 4\end{bmatrix}. \end{equation*}
    We define the following: 1. \(B=C((x-1)^4)\text{,}\) 2. \(C=C(x-1)\oplus C((x-1)^3)\text{,}\) 3. \(D=C(x-1)\oplus C(x-1)\oplus C((x-1)^2)\text{,}\) 4. \(E=C((x-1)^2)\oplus C((x-1)^2)\text{,}\) and 5. \(F=C(x-1)\oplus C(x-1)\oplus C(x-1)\oplus C(x-1)\text{.}\) As each of these matrices is in [[Theorem ‚Äì Rational Canonical Form|Rational Canonical Form]], they are explicit representatives for each similarity class.

    Problem F.181. Problem 7.

    Let \(L\) be the splitting field of \(x^5 - 11\) over \(\Q\)
    1. Find, with justification, the degree of \(L\) over \(\Q\text{.}\)
    2. Let \(F = \Q(\zeta)\) where \(\zeta = e^{2\pi i/5}\text{,}\) a primitive \(5\th\) root of unity. Prove \(x^5 - 11\) is irreducible in \(F[x]\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    Let \(L\) be the splitting field of \(f(x)=x^5 - 11\) over \(\Q\)
    Part (a) First, notice that by [[Theorem ‚Äì Eisenstein's Criterion|Eisenstein's Criterion]] (\(p=11\)) we have \(f\) irreducible in \(\Q\text{.}\)
    The roots of \(f\) are the following: 1. \(\a_1=\sqrt[5]{11}\z\) 2. \(\a_2=\sqrt[5]{11}\z^2\) 3. \(\a_3=\sqrt[5]{11}\z^3\) 4. \(\a_4=\sqrt[5]{11}\z^4\) 5. \(\a_5=\sqrt[5]{11}\z^5=\sqrt[5]{11}\)
    As \(f\) is monic and irreducible, it is the minimum polynomial of \(\a_5\text{.}\) Let \(E=\Q(\a_5)\) and notice \([E:\Q]=5\text{.}\)
    Notice that \(\z\text{,}\) a primitive \(5\)-th root of unity, is the root of \(g(x)=x^4+x^3+x^2+x+1\text{,}\) the fourth cyclotomic polynomial, which is monic and irreducible in \(\Q[x]\) (See: [[Proposition ‚Äì Cyclotomic Polynomial Irreducible|Result]]). Thus \(g\) is the minimum polynomial of \(\z\) and \([F:\Q]=4\text{.}\)
    We know that \(L=\Q(\sqrt[5]{11},\z)\text{,}\) as every generator of \(L\) can be written using \(\z\) and \(\sqrt[5]{11}.\) Let \([L:\Q]=n\text{,}\) \([L:F]=p\text{,}\) and \([L:E]=q\text{.}\) By the [[Proposition ‚Äì The Degree Formula|Degree Formula]], we have - \([L:\Q]=[L:F][F:\Q]\text{,}\) and thus \(n=4p\) - \([L:\Q]=[L:E][E:\Q]\text{,}\) and thus \(n=5q\) As \(E\neq L\) and \(F\neq L\) we know that \(p,q>1\text{.}\)
    Thus we have \(n=20\text{.}\)
    Part (b) Let \(F = \Q(\zeta)\) where \(\zeta = e^{2\pi i/5}\text{.}\) From Part (a) we know \([L:F]=5\text{.}\) As \(L=F(\a)\text{,}\) we know that \(\mp_\a(x)\) has degree \(5\text{.}\) As \(f\) is a monic polynomial of degree \(5\text{,}\) we see that it is the minimum polynomial and thus irreducible.

    Problem F.182. Problem 8.

    Let \(L\) be the splitting field of \(x^4-2022\) over \(\Q\text{.}\) Prove there exists a unique [[Mathematics/Definitions/Intermediate Field|intermediate]] field \(\Q\subseteq K\subseteq L\) such that \([K : \Q] = 4\) and \(\Q\subseteq K\) is a Galois extension.
    Solution.
    Let \(L\) be the splitting field of \(f(x)=x^4-2022\) over \(\Q\text{.}\) Let \(\z\) be a primitive fourth root of unity. Thus \(\z=e^{2\pi i/4}=e^{\pi/2}=\cos(\pi/2)+i\sin(\pi/2)=i\text{.}\)
    Notice that the roots of \(f\) are the following: - \(\sqrt[4]{2022} i\text{,}\) - \(\sqrt[4]{2022}i^2=-\sqrt[4]{2022}\text{,}\) - \(\sqrt[4]{2022}i^3=-\sqrt[4]{2022}i\text{,}\) and - \(\sqrt[4]{2022}i^4=\sqrt[4]{2022}\text{.}\) Thus \(L=\Q(\sqrt[4]{2022},i)\text{.}\)
    Using [[Theorem ‚Äì Eisenstein's Criterion|Eisenstein's Criterion]] with \(p=2\) we see that \(f\) is irreducible in \(\Q[x]\text{.}\) Let \(\a=\sqrt[4]{2022}\text{,}\) and notice that \(f\) is the minimum polynomial of \(\a\text{.}\) Let \(K=\Q(\a)\) and observe \([K:\Q]=4\text{.}\)
    As \(i\) is the root of the monic irreducible polynomial \(x^2+1\) we have \([L:F]=2\) and \([L:\Q]=[L:F][F:\Q]=2\cdot4=8\text{.}\) Thus \(G=\Gal(L/\Q)\) is isomorphic to a subgroup of \(S_4\) of order \(8\text{,}\) making it \(D_8\text{.}\)
    Notice that \(K\) is an extension of degree \(24\text{,}\) and thus by the [[Theorem ‚Äì Fundamental Theorem of Galois Theory|FTGT]] we have a subgroup \(H\leq G\) such that \(H=\Gal(L/K)\text{.}\) As \([L:\Q]=[L:K][K:\Q]\) we have \(8=[L:K]\cdot 4\text{,}\) and thus \(|H|=2\text{.}\) This makes \(H\) the cyclic subgroup of \(D_8\) generated by a reflection, the only element of order \(2\) in \(D_8\text{,}\) making it unique. This is also a normal subgroup of \(D_8\text{,}\) making \(K\) Galois over \(\Q\text{.}\)

    Problem F.183. Problem 9.

    Assume \(F\subseteq L\) is an algebraic field extension such that every non-constant polynomial in \(F[x]\) splits completely into linear factors in \(L[x]\text{.}\) Prove \(L\) is an [[Mathematics/Definitions/Algebraic Closure|algebraic closure]] of \(F\text{.}\)
    Solution.
    Let \(F\subseteq L\) be an algebraic field extension such that every non-constant polynomial in \(F[x]\) splits completely into linear factors in \(L[x]\text{.}\)
    Let \(f\) be a polynomial in \(L[x]\text{,}\) so \(f=a_nx^n+\dots+a_1x+a_0\text{,}\) with \(a_i\in L\text{,}\) and let \(\a\) be a root of \(f\text{.}\) There [[Theorem ‚Äì Existence and Uniqueness of Algebraic Closures|exists]] some field extension \(L\subseteq K\) such that \(K\) is algebraically closed, meaning \(\a\in K\text{.}\) Notice that \(\a\) is algebraic over \(L(\a)\text{.}\)
    Consider the chain of field extensions
    \begin{equation*} F\subseteq L\subseteq L(\a), \end{equation*}
    making \(\a\) algebraic over \(F\) as well. As every non-constant polynomial in \(F[x]\) splits completely into linear factors in \(L[x]\text{,}\) this yields \(\a\in L\text{.}\)
